# Excalidraw Data
## Text Elements
In this case, when we connect the multimeter with the DUT
to test its branch voltage, the equivalent resistance of the 
multimeter must be much larger than that of the DUT
or else due to what is illustrated in current divider model,
part of the current going through the DUT will be distributed
to the multimeter, which means the voltage of the DUT will
respectfully decrease, and thus the measurement result displayed
by the multimeter will be incorrect. ^awyroRri

When measuring the branch voltage of a device under test (DUT) 
using a multimeter, it's essential to connect the multimeter 
in parallel with the DUT. If the multimeter had low internal 
resistance, it would significantly affect the circuit operation
by drawing a considerable amount of current.

In parallel connections, the equivalent resistance of the circuit 
decreases. A multimeter with low internal resistance would create 
a low-resistance path, causing the majority of the current to flow 
through the multimeter rather than the DUT. This would lead to an 
inaccurate voltage reading because the voltage across the DUT would 
drop due to this additional load.

By designing the multimeter with a very high internal resistance, 
we minimize its impact on the circuit. This high resistance ensures 
that most of the current flows through the DUT, allowing the 
multimeter to measure the voltage accurately without affecting the 
circuit behavior. In essence, the large internal resistance of the 
multimeter allows it to act as a nearly invisible observer, ensuring 
reliable measurements while preserving the original conditions of 
the DUT. ^nLuBmq35

- Clear
- Has separate paragraphs
- Describes cause and effects ^aPRhaEeQ

- Simple
- Has graph
- Explains why the opposite way doesn't work ^d7sPc0z7
